Concluding Remarks
   The purpose of writing this book did not extend beyond discussing what is halal and what is haram in actions and outward behavior. As for the halal and the haram in the actions of the mind and the movements of the soul, it was not our intention to deal with them in this volume, even though such diseases of the soul as envy and covetousness, pride and arrogance, hypocrisy and ostentation, greed and lust, are among the major sins. Islam declares unrelenting war on them, and the Prophet (pbuh) warned of their evil consequences, characterizing some of them as "the disease of earlier nations" and calling them "the razor" which shaves not hair but religiosity.
   Anyone who studies the Qur'an and the Sunnah of the Prophet (pbuh) knows that soundness of the heart and integrity of the soul are considered the basis of success, both for the individual and for the society, in this world and in the Hereafter:
  Indeed, Allah does not change the condition of a people until they change what is in themselves...
(Ar-Ras d: 11)
 The Day on which (neither) wealth nor sons will be of no avail except to the one who brings to Allah a sound heart.
(Ash-Shux ara': 88-89)
Hence, the Prophet (pbuh) said,
"The halal is clear and the haram is clear. Between the two there are doubtful matters. Whoever avoids them in order to safeguard his religion and honor is safe, while whoever engages in a part of them may be doing something haranl, like one who grazes his animals near the hima it is thus quite likely that some of his animals will stray into it.

I . Hima refers to the grounds reserved for animals belonging to a king and which are out of bounds for others' animals.
Truly, even king has a hbna, and the hinta of Allah is what He has prohibited... "(1)
   He then explained the value of the heart, the source of the emotions, inclinations, and intentions which are the basis of all human behavior, saying,
"In the body there is a piece of flesh such that if it is sound the whole bodv is sound, while if it is corrupted the whole body is corrupted, and that is the heart.
   For, the heart is the chief organ of the body and rules it. If the ruler is good, the subjects are good, but if the ruler becomes corrupt, the subjects become corrupt.
   In the scale of Allah it is the heart and the intentions which have weight, not the face and the tongue:
"Allah does not look at your (physical) features, but He looks at your hearts.
"Deeds are judged by intentions, and everyone will be judged according to what he intends.
   Such is the place of the actions of the heart and the affairs of the soul in Islam. However, because they relate to the character of the individual rather than to what is lawful and what is prohibited, we have not dealt with them in this volume. These inner aspects of Islam have been discussed by other scholars and by Muslim Sufis in great depth and detail. They have referred to the moral and spiritual sins as "diseases of the heart," have diagnosed their causes, and have prescribed remedies for them in the light of the Book of Allah and the noble Sunnah of the Prophet (pbuh). Imam Al-Ghazali devoted one-fourth of his encyclopedic work about Islam, Ihya' \ Ulum ad-Din, to this topic, terming them "fatal diseases," since they lead a person to degradation in this world and to the Fire in the Hereafter.
   Another point to be noted is that we have dealt here only with sins of "commission" and not those of "omission," although sins are of two kinds: doing something which is forbidden and not doing something which is obligatory. The second type of sin was not the subject matter of this book, although we have touched upon it here and there in the course of discussion.

I . Narrated by Al-Bukhari, Muslim, and others; the narration is taken from At-Tirmidhi.
Had we aimed at describing the sins of omission, we would have been discussing an entirely different subject; we would then have been describing all the obligations which Allah has laid upon the Muslim, for unquestionably not doing them or neglecting them is haram. For example, seeking knowledge is an obligation on every Muslim, man and woman. If the Muslim keeps himself in the darkness of ignorance, he will commit sins through lack of knowledge and consequently will not carry out his religious obligations such as Prayer, Zakah, Fasting, and Hajj, which are pillars of Islam. It is not permissible for the Muslim to abandon them without a legitimate excuse since not to carry them out is a major sin, while to downgrade their value and deny them is to abandon Islam.
   To give another example, it is an obligation on the Muslim Ummah to muster milit'ary power to the utmost extent of its capacity in order to defend itself and to keep the enemy of Allah and Islam at bay. This is a general obligation on the entire community of Islam and, in particular, on those who are at the helm of affairs; accordingly, the neglect of such an important obligation is a major sin and great shame. We could go on multiplying example, since the case of all the other obligations, whether individual or collective, is similar.
   We do not claim to have covered the whole range of what is halal and what is haram. In these pages we have only highlighted the most important matters which are permissible or prohibited in the personal, family and social life of the Muslim, especially those matters which people neglect or consider insignificant due to their ignorance of the reasons behind a prohibition or permission. We have attempted to show the deep wisdom of the Islamic Shari\ah in its legislations concerning the halal and the haram, so that anyone, who possesses two eyes can observe that Allah (Glorified be He) is not arbitrary in His commandments, neither permitting things in order to be indulgent to people nor prohibiting them in order to make their lives miserable. Rather, He has legislated for them what is in their own best interest, safeguarding their lives, intellect, property, morals and honor, and guiding them toward success in this world and in the Hereafter.
   All man-made laws are inherently defective and incomplete, since the lawmakers, whether they be individuals, governments, or legislatures, limit themselves to dealing with material considerations, neglecting the demands
   
of religion and morality. They are forever confined within the narrow bounds of nationalism and the interests of a sector of mankind, paying little attention to the world at large and to the wider concerns of humanity; they legislate for the present, not knowing what lies in their future, nor what the effects of their laws will be. Over and above all this, legislators are human beings who are subject to their own weaknesses, desires, and prejudices Indeed, he (man) is unjust, ignorant. (Al-Ahzab: 72)) It is therefore not strange that man-made laws are short-sighted, shallow, and biased toward the material interests of the society, and that they provide only temporary remedies. It should not be surprising if human legislation concerning what is legal or illegal is to a great extent influenced by public opinion, by lobbyists, and by what is popular or unpopular; accordingly, things are often made legal even if it is known that they are extremely dangerous and evil in their consequences.
   One example of this may be sufficient. The government of the United States legalized drinking intoxicants, repealing the earlier legislation which had banned it. The repeal of prohibition was enacted despite full knowledge of the evil and harm which the consumption of intoxicants causes to individuals, families, and society. In contrast to this, the Sharrah of Islam is free of all such defects. How could it be otherwise when it was legislated by the All-Knowing Creator Who is aware of His creatures, what is suitable for them, and what they are capable of?
 ... Allah knows the one who makes corruption from him who sets things right...
(Al-Baqarah: 220)
 Should He not know Who created (all things)? And He is the Subtle, the Aware.
(Al-Mulk: 14)
   This is the legislation of the All-Wise God. He did not prohibit anything unnecessarily nor did He permit anything randomly. His creation is measured and His laws are harmonious.
   It is the legislation of the All-Merciful Lord. He desires ease for human beings and does not desire hardship for them. How could it be otherwise, when He is more Compassionate to His servants than the mothers who bore them?
346
   It is the legislation of the All-Powerful King. He is Independent of His servants and is not panial to one race or one generation, permitting to some what He prohibits to others. How could this be, when He is the Lord of all beings?
   This is what the Muslim believes concerning what Allah has made halal or haram and what He has legislated in relation to other aspects. He therelore accepts it with a convinced mind, a contented heart, and with a strong determination to implement it. Indeed, he believes that his happiness in this world and his success in the Hereafter depend entirely upon his observing the limits set by Allah, following His injunctions, and observing His prohibitions. In order to secure his happiness and success in both worlds, therefore, he must restrain himself from transgressing these limits.
   In order to illustrate how observant the first generation of Muslims was with regard to the limits set by Allah and how hard they strove to implement His commands, we cite two examples from their lives.
The First Example
   In our discussion concerning the prohibition of khamr, we mentioned how deeply the Arabs loved drinking intoxicants and how greatly they enjoyed drinking parties. Then Allah (Glorified be He) gradually turned them away from drinking until He revealed the decisive verse prohibiting khamr totally and forever, calling it "an abomination of Satan's doing" (Al-Matidah: 90). Thereupon the Prophet (pbuh) forbade Muslims to drink khamr, to sell it, or to give it as a gift to non-Muslims.
   When the Muslims heard about the prohibition of khamr, they at once brought out their stocks of intoxicants and poured them into the streets of Madinah as a declaration that they had abandoned drinking. Even more remarkable in expressing their total submission to what Allah had legislated was the action of those who were engaged in drinking khamr when the verse, Will you not then desist?" (Al-Ma'idah: 91) reached them. Without hesitating for a moment, they threw whatever drinks were left in their glasses upon the ground, exclaiming, "O Lord, we have desisted!"
   When we compare this manifest success in combating and eradicating intoxicants from the Islamic society with the disastrous failure of the

government of the United which decided one day to combat this evi( by means of laws and force, we cannot escape the obvious conclusion that human beings do not reform themselves except by means of the Shari'ah of Allah, which relies on the faith and the consciences of the people before endeavoring to apply authority and force.
The Second Example
   This concerns the response of the first generation of Muslim women to All'ah's prohibiting women the display of their adornment and enjoining modesty and the covering of their Nawrah. During the period of Jahiliyyah women used to go about with their hair, neck, and breasts uncovered, displaying the ornaments they wore such as earrings and necklaces. Allah (Glorified be He) then prohibited Muslim women this wanton display of the period of ignorance, commanding them to be different and distinguishable from the women of Jahiliyyah by observing modesty and by drawing their head-coverings over their hair, neck, and bosoms in order to conceal them.
   The following are narrations from the Mother of the Believers, %A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with her), concerning the manner in which the women of the Muhajirun and the Ansar received the divine injunction which required a major change in their life-styles, affecting their appearance, dress, and ornamentation. She said, "May Allah shower His mercy on the early women of the Muhqjirun. When the verse, € that they should draw their head-coverings over their bosoms... (An-Nur: 31) was revealed, they tore up their garments to cover themselves.
   Once when some women were in the company of A A'ishah, they mentioned the women of the Quraysh and their merits. N A lishah remarked,
   "The Qurayshi women were indeed good, but, by Allah, I have seen no one better than the women of the Ansar in applying the Book of Allah and believing in the revelation. When the verse of Surat an-Nur, that they should draw their head-coverings over their bosoms..., " was revealed, their men returned to their homes and recited it to their women. No sooner did the

l. A more detailed discussion of this comparison is available in our book A/- 'Aqidah Darurah lil-Havah, in the section on belief and morals.
2. Narrated by Al-Bukhari,
348
man recite it to his wife, his daughter, his sister, or any other female relative, then she tied any piece of cloth (available to her, perhaps) from a curtain having pictures on it on her head, so that when they came to offer prayer behind the Prophet (pbuh), it looked as if crows were sitting on their heads. l)
   This was the response of the believing women to what Allah had legislated for them. They hastened to implement what He had commanded and to abandon what He had prohibited without hesitating, stopping to think about it, or waiting. They did not even delay a day or two so that they could get a suitable, soft piece of cloth and sew it to fit their heads and cover their bosoms; rather, any cloth which was available, whether rough, brightly-colored, or having pictures on it, was sufficiently soft and befitting. If no other cloth was found, they simply tore up the garments they were wearing and tied the strips to their heads, not caring about their appearance, which, as the Mother of the Believers described it, resembled that of crows sitting on their heads.
   The point we wish to stress here is that mere academic knowledge of the halal and the haram, and of the limits of these is not sufficient. Although the major sins and principal obligations are generally known to every Muslim, nonetheless we find a great many of them indulging in these sins and neglecting those obligations, and rushing toward the fire with their eyes wide open.
   If the Muslim is well-versed in the knowledge of his religion and its Sharüah, and at the same time possesses a fully alert conscience which safeguards the limits so that they cannot be overstepped, he is indeed rich in all goodness. The Prophet (pbuh) spoke the truth when he said,
"When Allah intends good jbr a person, He makes his own soul a vvatcher over him.
   We end our book with the following supplication which has come to us from the early Muslims:

l. Ibn Kathir mentioned this in his discussion concerning this verse, on the authority of Ibn Abi Hatim.
2. Al? Iraqi said, "Ad-Daylami narrated it in Musnad al-Firdaws on good authority.

"O Allah, make us independent of Your haram with Your halal, of disobedience to You with obedience to You, and of any other than You with Your bounty!"
   All praise is due to Allah (Glorified and Exalted be He), Who has guided us to this; had He not given us guidance, we would not have been guided!

Glossary
)4wrah: A part of one's body, which is illegal to keep naked before others.
Ansar: The Companions of the Prophet (pbuh) from the inhabitants of Madinah who embraced and supported Islam and who received and entertained the Muslim emigrants who had emigrated from Makkah and other places.
Dhimmi: A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic government.
*Id: It signifies any of the al-Fitr (Lesser Bairam or Minor Feast) or the Vd al-Adhha (Greater Bairam or Major Feast).
Fatwa (pl. fatawa): A legal judgment generally issued by a scholar of Fiqh (Islamic Jurisprudence).
Fiqh: The science dealing with Islamic jurisprudence in all aspects, worship, marital affairs, and transactions, etc.
Fiqhi (sometimesfiqhiyyah): Pertaining to Fiqh:
Ghusl: Washing the whole body in ceremonial way. This is obligatory for one who is in a state of Janabah (the state of a person after having sexual intercourse with his wife or after having a seminal discharge in a wet dream), and also on other occasions.
Hadd: A punishment specified by the Shari*ah, such as the punishments of adultery, fornication, theft, and wine-drinking.
Hadith: Any of the traditions of the Prophet (pbuh), which contain his sayings and practices. It also refers to the science concerned with these traditions; this is always used with capital initial (Hadith).
Hadith Qudsi: Hadith in which the Prophet (pbuh) told something from Allah the Almighty. In the Prophetic hadith, the chain of authorities ends with the Prophet (pbuh), while in the Hadith Qudsi, the final attribution is to Allah the Almighty.

   
Hajj: Pilgrimage to Makkah.
Halal: That which is permitted, with respect to which no restriction exists, and the doing of which the Law-Giver, Allah, has allowed.
Haram: That which the Law-Giver has absolutely prohibited.
Hasan: When a lladilh is classified as hasan it means that this hadith has the requirements of the hadith which is classified as sahih, except that one of its narrators was weak in memorization and inaccurate.
Iddah: A woman's waiting period after divorce or after her husband's death.
Ihram: Ihram refers to the state of consecration, physical and spiritual, of the Muslim who is performing Hajj or \Umrah.
Ijtihad: Personal Reasoning: a secondary source of Islamic Law.
Imam: A term with numerous connotations, all revolving about the idea of "leadership .
Injil: The (undistorted) Gospel that was revealed to Prophet (Jesus), peace be upon him.
Istikharah: A prayer consisting of two raka sat in which the praying person appeals to Allah to guide him on the right way, regarding a certain deed or situation with which one is confronted. (See Hadith No. 263, vol. 2, Hadith No.391, vol. 8, Hadith No. 487, Vol. 9, Sahih al-Bukhari).
Jahili: Pertaining to Jahiliyyah.
Jahiliyyah: This term is used to signify the pre-Islamic period. Literally, it means ignorance. It may also be used to denote a perpetual state of ignorance as compared with the ignorance that prevailed during the pre-Islamic period.
Jihad: Fighting in the cause of Allah or any kind of effort to keep Allah's word superior, which is regarded as one of the principles of Islam.
Jizyah: Head-tax imposed by Islam on dhimmis (non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic government).
Köbah: A square stone building in Al-Masjid-al-Haram (the great mosque at Makkah) towards which all Muslims turn their faces in prayer.
Khamr: Anything that intoxicates, such as wine and the like.
Khalwah: Khalwah denotes a man and woman's being alone together in a place where there is no fear of intrusion by anyone else, so that an opportunity exists for sexual intimacy that may lead to zina.
Mahram: A mahram relationship denotes a relationship either by marriage or by close blood ties of such degree that marriage is permanently prohibited. With reference to a woman, a mahram is either her husband or any male relative to whom marriage is permanently prohibited, such as her father, grandfather, son, brother, uncle, or nephew.
Makruh: That which is disapproved by the Law-Giver but not very strongly. The makruh is less in degree than the haram (prohibited), and one who commits it is not punished as he is punished for a haram act, except when he does it in excess or in a manner that leads to what is haram.
Mawla: In Arabic, this word is used for many meanings such as friend, fellow, client, servant, and freed man.
Muhajirun: This is the plural of muhajir, a term that refers to anyone of the early Muslims who had emigrated from any place to Madinah in the lifetime of the Prophet (pbuh) before the conquest of Makkah and also the one who quits things that Allah has forbidden.
Mushrik: Adjective and noun signifying anyone who commits shirk.
Mut*ah Marriage: A personal contract between a man and a woman to cohabit for a limited period of time at the end of which she becomes automatically divorced, in return for a certain remuneration payable by the man. It may be generally referred to as "temporary marriage"
Muttafaq •Alayh: A hadith (or a tradition) upon which Al-Bukhari and Muslim are agreed is described as muttafaq malayh (literally meaning "agreed upon").
Pbuh: Peace be upon him.
Quraysh: One of the greatest tribes in Arabia in the Pre-Islamic Period of Ignorance. Prophet Muhammad belonged to this tribe, which had great powers spiritually and financially both before and after Islam came.
RaEah (pl. raköat): The prayer of Muslims consists of raköat (singular-rak'ah). A rak\ah consists of one standing, one bowing, and two acts of prostration.
Riba: In Islam, riba (commonly known as usury or interest) is of two major kinds: (a) Riba an-Nasi fah, i.e. interest on lent money; (b) Riba al-Fadl, i.e. taking a superior thing of the same kind of goods by giving more of the same kind of goods of inferior quality, e.g., dates of superior quality for dates of inferior quality in greater amount. Islam strictly forbids all kinds of riba.
Sahih: When a hadith is classified as sahih it means that this hadith was narrated by a purely authentic chain of transmitters from the beginning to the end. Also, the word sahih (always with capital initial), when referring to a book, means that the contents of this book are "authentic" collections of hadiths and traditions, e.g., Sahih al-Bukhari.
Sharrah•. Islamic law, which contains all the ordinances that Allah the Almighty has prescribed for Muslims.
Shirk: Associating anyone (or anything) with Allah. It may be generally expressed by "polytheism".
Sunnah: All the traditions and practices of the Prophet (pbuh) that have become a model to be followed by Muslims.
Surah: Any of the chapters of the Qur'an.
Tawrah: The (undistorted) Torah that was revealed to Prophet Musa (Moses), peace be upon him.
Tözir: For crimes concerning which no specified punishment is mentioned in the Qur'an or the Sunnah, the Muslim judge (or government) may introduce discretionary punishments, such as fines or imprisonment. This is ta szir.
Tawhid: Believing in the Oneness of Allah.
Ummah: This word refers to the Muslim nations and communities all over the world, as one entity.
Umrah: A visit to Makkah at any time of the year during which one performs the circumambulation around the Ka\bah and going for seven times between the mountains of As-Safa and Al-Marwah. It may be called "The Lesser Pilgrimage"
Zakah: A certain fixed proportion of the wealth and of the each and every kind of the property liable to Zakah of a Muslim to be paid yearly for the benefit of the poor in the Muslim community. The payment of Zakah is obligatory, as it is one of the five pillars of Islam.
Zaniyat (sing. zaniyah): An Arabic term in the plural form signifying both adulteresses and fornicatresses.
Zina: The Arabic term "zina" signifies adultery or fornication.
